further ˈfɜːrðər Further means at or from a greater distance or time. The escalator is further than I thought.

neither ˈniːðə(r) You use neither to connect two negative possibilities. Neither the path on the left nor the path on the right will lead us home.
otherwise ˈʌðərwaɪz Otherwise means in another way if you don’t do this. It’s good to stay active; otherwise, you’ll gain weight.

cynical ˈsɪnɪkl To be cynical is to be unwilling to believe that people have good intentions. The man became cynical after being lied to many times.

glamour ˈɡlæmər Glamour is a quality relating to riches, wealth, and beauty. The young woman showed of her glamour during the photo shoot.

essay ˈeseɪ An essay is a short piece of writing on a certain subject. She had to write a two-page essay for her English class.
evaluate ɪˈvæljueɪt To evaluate something means to study it, so a decision can be made. Jo and Ken evaluated the plan and agreed that it would work.
faint feɪnt To faint means to go unconscious and fall down. Carol fainted because she hadn’t eaten in over a day.
global ˈɡləʊbl If something is global, it happens all around the world. Pollution is a global problem.

private ˈpraɪvət If something is private, it is only used by one person or group. Don’t look at my diary! It’s private!
recent ˈriːsnt When something is recent, it happened a short time ago. I did not know what had caused his recent behavior.

degree dɪˈɡriː Degree is a unit for measuring temperature. The thermometer recorded a temperature of 38 degrees Celsius.
exterior ɪkˈstɪriər An exterior is the outside surface of something. The exterior of the nut was hard and woody, but the inside was soft and delicious.
hearty ˈhɑːrti If someone or something is hearty, then they are loud and happy. The grandmother ended her story with a hearty laugh that pleased her grandchild.
hospitable ˈhɒspɪtəbl If someone is hospitable, they are friendly to strangers. At dinner, my dad was very hospitable to my friends.
humor ˈhjuːmər Humor can be something that makes you laugh, and it can also refer to your feelings. He woke up in an ill humor, angry at everyone.
manor ˈmænər A manor is a large house with many rooms. The manor had over forty rooms and beautiful gardens.
monastery ˈmɑːnəsteri A monastery is a building in which monks live. The monks at this monastery are famous for their bread and music.
nursery ˈnɜːrsəri A nursery is a room where babies and children sleep, play, or are cared for. The children kissed their parents goodnight and went upstairs to the nursery.
outstretched ˌaʊtˈstretʃt If something is outstretched, then it is extended to its full length. The cat stood on its back legs with its front legs outstretched.
parcel ˈpɑːrsl A parcel is a package of things to be carried or mailed somewhere. The parcel looked like it had been stepped on by someone.
profile ˈprəʊfaɪl A profile is an outline of a face, usually as seen from the side. The drawing of Olivia’s profile came out very well.
winding ˈwaɪndɪŋ If something is winding, then it follows a twisting course. The winding river turned and looped around the hills.
zip zɪp To zip something means to close it with a zipper. She zipped her backpack closed after putting her books inside it.
abolish əˈbɑːlɪʃ To abolish something means to put an end to it, such as a system or law. President Lincoln abolished slavery in the US.
amend əˈmend To amend something means to change it to improve or make it accurate. The countries were in agreement that the treaty needed to be amended.
aspire əˈspaɪər To aspire means to have a strong desire to achieve or do something. George aspired to be a doctor from a young age.
censor ˈsensər To censor information means to remove it if it is dangerous, rude, or rebellious. To protect innocent people, the location of the bomb was censored.
charter ˈtʃɑːrtər A charter is a document that describes the rights of an organization or group. The company charter explained that all employees had to pay a tax.
constitution ˌkɑːnstɪˈtuːʃn A constitution is a document of principles for a government. The country’s constitution said a prime minister could only serve three terms.
cosmopolitan ˌkɑːzməˈpɑːlɪtən When a place is cosmopolitan, it is full of people from many different places. There are dozens of different types of restaurants in a cosmopolitan city.
disseminate dɪˈsemɪneɪt To disseminate information or knowledge means to distribute it. The organization disseminates information about the dangers of smoking.
flatter ˈflætər To fatter people means to praise them in an effort to please them. He was just flattering me when he said that my new dress looked gorgeous.
infamous ˈɪnfəməs When someone is infamous, they are well known for something bad. That news channel is infamous for presenting biased information.
lame leɪm If one is lame, they cannot walk properly due to an injury to the leg or foot. The terrible accident left many people dead and several others lame.
limp lɪmp To limp means to walk with difficulty because someone’s leg or foot is hurt. After the injury, the player limped off of the field.
outburst ˈaʊtbɜːrst An outburst is a sudden, strong expression of an emotion. There was an outburst of cheers when the comedian took the stage.
pathological ˌpæθəˈlɑːdʒɪkl When a behavior is pathological, it is extreme, unacceptable, and sometimes a symptom of disease. The pathological liar could not even tell the truth about unimportant matters.
phenomenal fəˈnɑːmɪnl When something is phenomenal, it is unusually great. The child’s ability to play the piano is nothing short of phenomenal.
poll pəʊl A poll is a survey in which people give their opinions about important things. The poll showed that many people support the plan to stop gang violence.
remorse rɪˈmɔːrs Remorse is a strong feeling of sadness and regret. When I realized what I had done, I felt remorse for my actions.
secrecy ˈsiːkrəsi Secrecy is the behavior of keeping things secret. The secrecy of the big organization made the government nervous.
tackle ˈtækl To tackle something means to deal with it in a determined and efficient way. Such social problems need to be tackled right away.
trance træns A trance is a state where people seem asleep and have no control of themselves. The woman’s powerful eyes often put men in a trance.
coward ˈkaʊərd A coward is a person who lacks courage to do risky or dangerous things. A firefighter cannot be a coward. They have to be able to act quickly.
delete dɪˈliːt To delete something means to remove or erase written material. Several lines had been deleted from her speech.
earnest ˈɜːrnɪst If someone is earnest, then they are honest. The child was very earnest when she told her mother how she broke the dish.
ethnic ˈeθnɪk If something is ethnic, then it is related to a group with a similar culture. Many sections of the city are home to different ethnic communities.
exclude ɪkˈskluːd To exclude someone means to not accept them into a group. Carol was excluded from the contest because her friend was a judge.
firsthand fə́ːrsthǽnd If something is firsthand, then it is from an original source. If you want firsthand knowledge, ask someone who saw it.
fluent ˈfluːənt If someone is fluent in a language, then they are able to speak it very well. She was so fluent in German that you’d have thought she was from Germany.
imperial ɪmˈpɪriəl If something is imperial, then it is related to an empire. These old imperial coins were once used in the Roman Empire.
inclusive ɪnˈkluːsɪv If something is inclusive, then it is open to all groups and people in society. A more inclusive event would have allowed children to attend.
legislature ˈledʒɪsleɪtʃər A legislature is the section of a government that makes laws. The senator had served ten years in the national legislature.
linguistic lɪŋˈɡwɪstɪk If something is linguistic, then it is concerned with language. A linguistic way of studying culture focuses on words within that culture.
monolingual ˌmɑːnəˈlɪŋɡwəl If someone is monolingual, then they speak only one language. In today’s global economy, being monolingual limits your opportunities.
nationality ˌnæʃəˈnæləti Nationality is an identity based on the nation from which you come. His nationality is German, but he speaks French, Spanish, and Korean.
patriot ˈpeɪtriət A patriot is someone who loves, supports, and defends their country. Every year, young patriots join their countries’ militaries.
prosecute ˈprɑːsɪkjuːt To prosecute someone means to take legal action against them. They were prosecuted for fishing in the river without a permit.
racial ˈreɪʃl If something is racial, then it is related to a race or races. The differences between racial groups are physical characteristics and culture.
solemn ˈsɑːləm If something is solemn, then it is serious and honest. The professor preferred a solemn relationship between him and his students.
solidarity ˌsɑːlɪˈdærəti Solidarity is a union formed from common responsibilities or interests. All the citizens came together in a show of solidarity to create change.
tact tækt Tact is the ability to avoid offending people when dealing with problems. Since both sides would not agree, it required someone with tact to make peace.
undermine ˌʌndərˈmaɪn To undermine someone means to betray them or weaken their efforts or authority. The documents helped to undermine the workers’ trust in their bosses’ honesty.
constrain kənˈstreɪn To constrain something means to limit its development. Jim cannot join us because he is constrained by previous plans.
depot ˈdiːpəʊ A depot is a bus or train station. He waited for his mother to arrive at the depot.
emulate ˈemjuleɪt To emulate people means to imitate them because they are greatly admired. As a small boy, he always tried to emulate his big brother.
forefinger ˈfɔːrfɪŋɡər The forefinger is the finger between one’s thumb and middle finger. He shouted, “There it is!” and pointed with his forefinger.
guts gʌts The guts are all the organs inside a person or animal. The doctor can tell you every process that happens in one’s guts.
inherent ɪnˈhɪərənt When something is inherent, it is a natural part of something else. Sweating is an inherent bodily function when exercising.
intimidate ɪnˈtɪmɪdeɪt To intimidate means to frighten others. My dad intimidates my friends whenever they visit.
janitor ˈdʒænɪtər A janitor is a person who makes repairs and takes care of a building. The school janitor cleaned up the messy cafeteria.
moist mɔɪst When something is moist, it is slightly wet. The ground is still moist from the rain last night.
nope nəʊp Nope is an informal way of saying “no.” He asked if I had any money, and I had to say, “Nope.”
prod prɑːd To prod means to push someone or something with a finger or pointed object. The bully prodded me in the chest with her finger.
ransom ˈrænsəm A ransom is a sum of money paid to a kidnapper to set the person free. He kidnapped the prince and demanded $1 million as a ransom.
restrain rɪˈstreɪn To restrain someone or something means to use physical strength to stop them. Mike restrained Allen from reaching the door.
saliva səˈlaɪvə Saliva is the watery liquid in people’s mouths that helps in digestion. The baby could not keep the saliva from dripping out of its mouth.
spit spɪt To spit means to force liquid from one’s mouth. He emerged from the pool and spit water from his mouth.
sprint sprɪnt To sprint means to run very fast over a short distance. The kids didn’t want to be late to class, so they sprinted to the bus stop.
stunt stʌnt A stunt is something that is done in order to get attention or publicity. The man jumped over the cars as a promotional stunt.
tolerant ˈtɑːlərənt When people are tolerant, they are respectful of others’ rights and beliefs. The tolerant leader thought that everyone was equal regardless of race.
vampire ˈvæmpaɪər A vampire is a fictional monster that sleeps in a coffin and sucks people’s blood. The vampire snuck up on the woman and bit her neck.
yawn jɔːn To yawn means to open one’s mouth wide and breathe in air. The child yawned and stretched her arms before going to bed for the night.
adjoining əˈdʒɔɪnɪŋ If something is adjoining, it is next to or joined with a building or room. I couldn’t sleep because the people in the adjoining room were loud.
allege əˈledʒ To allege something is to say that it is true without offering proof. The little girl had alleged that her older brother hid her favorite doll.
arch ɑːrtʃ An arch is a curved opening formed under a structure such as a bridge or doorway. The arch of the bridge was not high enough for the tall boat to pass underneath.
assemble əˈsembl To assemble means to get together in one place. The parents assembled to discuss ways to improve their children’s education.
casualty ˈkæʒuəlti A casualty is a person killed or injured in a war or an accident. The only casualty in the car accident was a woman who broke her arm.
erect ɪˈrekt To erect something means to build it. The king erected two towers on the north and south sides of his castle.
foul faʊl If something is foul, then it is very unpleasant. He wouldn’t let his dog drink from the water because it had a foul smell.
hectare ˈhektɑː(r) A hectare is a unit of measure equal to 10,000 square meters. His family farm covered many hectares.
heighten ˈhaɪtn To heighten an emotion means to increase the intensity of it. The pleasant music heightened their enjoyment of the wonderful dinner.
hospitality ˌhɑːspɪˈtæləti Hospitality is friendly behavior and entertainment, shown to guests or strangers. The travelers were amazed at the hospitality given them by the hotel’s staff.
mansion ˈmænʃn A mansion is a large and expensive home. The mansion had thirty bedrooms, two kitchens, and a pool.
outnumber ˌaʊtˈnʌmbər To outnumber a group means to have a greater number than it. The girls outnumbered the boys at the school by four to one.
overjoyed ˌəʊvərˈdʒɔɪd If someone is overjoyed, then they are extremely happy. He was overjoyed by the news of his promotion.
pasture ˈpæstʃər A pasture is an area of land covered with grass for animals to use as food. The sheep were taken to a pasture where there was more grass.
petition pəˈtɪʃn A petition is a written request asking an authority to do something. The citizens all signed a petition asking the mayor to repair the sidewalks.
renovate ˈrenəveɪt To renovate a building means to repair it, or to build new structures on it. The old fire station was renovated into an apartment building.
revise rɪˈvaɪz To revise something means to change it, or update it to make it better. When the editor discovered certain facts were wrong, he revised the book.
slab slæb A slab is a large, thick, flat piece of stone, concrete, metal, or wood. I looked at various slabs of stone to decorate my house.
terrace ˈterəs A terrace is an open area that is connected to a house or an apartment. In the afternoons, she liked to sit on the terrace and check her email.
turf tɜːrf Turf is a section of grass and the dirt in which it grows. After the game, the turf looked ragged.
analogous əˈnæləɡəs If something is analogous to another thing, then it is like it in certain ways. The relationship with his teacher was analogous to that of a son and mother.
binoculars bɪˈnɑːkjələrz Binoculars are a device used for seeing things that are far away. He could see the ship on the horizon only if he used his binoculars.
bulk bʌlk The bulk of something is its size. The large elephant moved its bulk with legs as strong as tree trunks.
comprise kəmˈpraɪz If something comprises something else, it consists of or is made up of it. Our school’s football team is mostly comprised with seniors.
depict dɪˈpɪkt To depict something means to show or portray it, often using art. The statue’s face depicted the general’s determination and courage.
dual ˈduːəl If something is dual, then it is made up of two parts. The room had a dual function. It was a living room, but at night it was a bedroom.
Fahrenheit fǽrənhàit Fahrenheit is a temperature scale where water freezes at 32° and boils at 212°. When the temperature dropped to 32° Fahrenheit, it started snowing.
fulfill fulfíl To fulfill something means to achieve or finish it. The professor did not fulfill his promise not to miss a single class.
grove ɡrəʊv A grove is a small group of trees. All the trees in this grove are apple trees.
ore ɔːr Ore is the raw form of rock or material from which a valuable metal is taken. The factory melted the ore and used it to make iron products.
outback ˈaʊtbæk The outback is the wild inland region of Australia where very few people live. Many Australian farmers use the outback to raise cattle.
outweigh ˌaʊtˈweɪ To outweigh something means to exceed it in value, amount, or importance. Finding a warm place to sleep outweighed the need to find something to eat.
paradox ˈpærədɑːks A paradox is a true statement or real event that seems illogical. The paradox of her work was that the less she worked, the more she got done.
pier pɪr A pier is a structure that extends into a body of water. If you want to catch bigger fish, then go to the far end of the pier.
shortcut ˈʃɔːrtkʌt A shortcut is a route that is shorter than the main route. We got to the house early because we took a shortcut through the forest.
tariff ˈtærɪf A tariff is a tax or fee paid on certain imports or exports. Our tariff on imported wool makes foreign wool more expensive.
thermometer θərˈmɑːmɪtər A thermometer is a device that measures temperature. The thermometer outside the window indicated that it was a hot day today.
tilt tɪlt To tilt something means to tip it into a sloping position. She tilted her glass and almost spilled some of the wine inside.
vice versa váisə və́ːrsə If a statement is vice versa, then its two main ideas are switched with one another. Students learn from their teachers, and vice versa.
whereabouts ˈwerəbaʊts The whereabouts of someone or something is the place where they are. The police looked for the lost dog, but its whereabouts were still unknown.
